For a half-exterior, half-interior condition with diarrhea and thirst, along with water regurgitation, use Wu Ling San. If there is severe heat and thirst, use Yi Yuan San to clear it. If both the exterior and interior heat are severe, use Jiedu Tang and Shen Xiong Wan, or wine-steamed Huang Lian. For individuals who are typically weak, or elderly persons suffering from summer heat with weak pulse and diarrhea, and who crave warmth or exhibit syncope, use Zhu Ye Shi Gao Tang with half a piece of Fu Zi taken cold. During summer, one must avoid all hot and drying agents. If misused, it can lead to symptoms of jaundice with small urine output, confusion, and possibly death.

    When distinguishing between damage from summer heat and cold damage, both present with fever but should be clearly identified. Cold damages the body's form, while summer heat damages qi. Cold damage is characterized by aversion to cold with a floating and tense pulse, while summer heat exhibits aversion to heat with a weak and faint pulse—this is the difference. The classics state: a strong pulse with cold body indicates cold damage; a weak pulse with hot body indicates summer heat damage. The treatment should involve Xiao Chai Hu Tang with added Shi Gao and Zhi Mu, or the use of Ren Shen Bai Hu Tang. In times of excessive rain and dampness coupled with cold, use Cang Zhu Bai Hu Tang. For those whose original qi is weak but suffering from severe damage, use Qing Shu Yi Qi Tang.[50p]
    Heatstroke

    Heatstroke manifests as a weak and suppressed pulse, body heat with a cold back, a dirty face and spontaneous sweating, restlessness and extreme thirst, hair standing on end with a dislike for cold, drowsiness and fatigue without body pain, differing from symptoms of cold damage. If there is internal and external heat, with dry mouth and thirst, limbs slightly cold without body pain, use Baihu Decoction. For phlegm rebellion and aversion to cold, use Orange Peel Decoction. If there is heat and oppression without aversion to cold, use Bamboo Leaf and Gypsum Decoction. For headache, nausea and irritability, discomfort in the heart area, and difficulty in urination, use Wuling Powder and Xiaoshu Wan for heat relief. For heatstroke, Xiao Chai Hu Decoction is the best. With a thin, slow pulse, open pores and fine hair standing, dry mouth and tongue, use Baihu Decoction with Ginseng. For cholera, restlessness, extreme thirst, abdominal pain, cold limbs, and leg cramping, use Huang Lian and Xiang Ru Decoction.

    When suffering from “three yang” heat during summer, the ailment must be heat-related; if the pulse is rapid, modify the Xuming Decoction by removing Aconite, Cinnamon, and Ephedra, and adding Huang Qin, Huang Lian, and Chai Hu. If there is a serious heat spike in summer with headache, irritability, thirst, a cold back, slight sweating, and a weak, deficient pulse, it is called heatstroke, for which cool and refreshing medicines should be used.

    When applying remedies for heat conditions, if Xiang Ru is included, first decoct Xiang Ru to boil for a moment, then add the remaining herbs to decoct together.[51p]
    Principles of Diagnosis and Treatment
    (Ming Dynasty - Wang Kentang)

    Heat Illnesses in Summer

    It is noted that heat illnesses such as summer heatstroke, heat exhaustion, and heat sickness, though they have different names, are in fact the same condition. If one is harmed by cold in winter and later develops a heat illness in summer, this transforms due to a delayed response, indicating a disease that has moved from the inside to the surface of the body, which is commonly referred to as a late onset. This differs from the acute onset of new heatstroke. It is said that in cases of new heatstroke, the pulse is weak, whereas in cases of late-onset heat illness, the pulse is strong.[52p]
    Differentiating Between Heat Stroke and Cold Damage

    Zhang said: "The clear pathogens enter from above, while the turbid pathogens enter from below. Wind, cold, and dampness are all the energies of the earth and are associated with turbid pathogens, which is why they affect the channels of the lower body. Only summer heat is the energy of heaven, associated with clear pathogens, affecting the hand's lesser yin heart channel. The symptoms often resemble those of cold damage, but the pulse of heat stroke differs from that of cold damage. Although cold damage is characterized by aversion to cold and fever, in its early stages, it does not lead to thirst. In contrast, with heat stroke, thirst arises immediately upon onset, distinguishing it from cold damage. Furthermore, the pulse in cold damage is typically strong and floating, while in heat stroke, it is weak and may be wiry, thin, or slow. The classics state: 'A strong pulse with a cold body indicates cold damage; a weak pulse with a hot body indicates heat stroke.'

    For instance, if there is a condition resembling yang disease, with rigidity in the neck and back, sweating, and aversion to wind, during the hot summer days, would it not seem similar to heat stroke? However, the absence of thirst differentiates it, thus it is treated with Gui Zhi Jia Ge Gen Tang. Regardless of whether one resides in summer or autumn during hot weather, one should carefully follow the texts, for there is no worry of error.

    In terms of symptoms, cold damage has aversion to cold, while heat damage has aversion to heat; in terms of pulse, cold damage presents with a strong pulse, while heat stroke shows a weak pulse. Additionally, the weak and thin pulse of heat stroke has some similarities with the pulse of damp constriction, but the symptoms differ. With heat, there is spontaneous sweating and thirst; with dampness, there is no thirst; and with constriction, there is body pain.**[53p]
    The pulse is weak and the body is hot due to sunstroke.

    Liu Chun stated: According to Scholar Xu, the pulse when harmed by heat is thin, fine, and weak—why is this? The "Inner Canon" states: cold injures the form, while heat injures the qi. Thus, if the qi is harmed but not the form, the qi depletes and the pulse weakens. The terms thin, fine, and weak all refer to a deficient pulse. Zhongjing identified the thin pulse as indicating yin, and Zhu Gong also mentioned that a pulse weak and fine corresponds to sunstroke, confirming that it is indeed a deficient pulse.

    Sunstroke resembles the manifestations of heat illness, but in heat illness the pulse is robust, whereas in sunstroke, the pulse is weak; this is the distinguishing factor. Additionally, damp warmth is similar to sunstroke, but the difference lies in the absence of thirst when the body feels cool.[54p]
    The Complete Collection of Ancient and Modern Books on Medicine, Volume 232 – Heat Ailments

    Medical Laws – Qing Dynasty, Yu Chang

    Heat Stroke

    Heat stroke is the term used for those who suffer from heat illness. According to the "Zuo Zhuan," it has long been known that people are shaded under trees to avoid heat. In later generations, those who become ill due to movement are said to have heat exhaustion, while those who suffer while at rest are considered to have heat stroke. Thus, can we say that those who suffer from heat while traveling are experiencing it at rest? The terms "movement" and "rest" can only distinguish between external influences and internal injuries. If one becomes ill due to movement, it is an external reaction to the heat of the sun; if one becomes ill due to resting, it is an injury from avoiding the sun’s heat only to suffer from dampness, wind, dew, and the consumption of cold fruits. During the seasons of minor and major cold, those who are affected suffer from cold illnesses; during the minor and major heat seasons, those who are affected suffer from heat illnesses. Laborious people, exposed to cold and heat, are thus more prone to cold and heat-related illnesses. Conversely, those who live in comfort and do not expose themselves to cold or heat through diet and activity invite no such ailments. Therefore, the treatment varies based on food and geography. Individuals with excessive internal dampness are most susceptible to heat stroke, as both external heat and internal dampness interact and cause it.[55p]
    Therefore, those who are overweight and have excess moisture, during the summer months try many methods to avoid the heat, but if they cannot avoid the moisture in their bodies, they will not be able to escape the heat of the heavens either. The Yiyuan Powder drives out dampness through urination, and taking it in the summer alleviates heat, and this has been known for a long time. However, it is suitable for those whose bodies are full of dampness. For those with a lean and moist-free physique, their bodily fluids are consumed by seasonal changes, and they should use the Shengmai Powder to replenish their fluids. If they use Yiyuan recklessly to promote urination, they will deplete their lower sources, leading to dryness and deprivation. Moreover, the dampness induced by summer heat often coexists with underlying cold in overweight individuals, which can lead to severe symptoms such as vomiting and diarrhea, cold sweats, and life-threatening conditions. Relying solely on Yiyuan Powder to relieve heat and drive out dampness often exacerbates the situation, ultimately compromising organ function. Is it not crucial to discern its use with care? Furthermore, whenever there is excessive sweating, one should avoid promoting urination. This is vital, as the only bodily fluids in the stomach are this moisture. Once it leaks out and flows downward, it can be termed an approach that leads to depletion. Zhongjing referred to this condition as "no yang," and if their pulse is short, rapid, and weak, that indicates a departure from vitality. Sudden heatstroke can lead to collapse and loss of consciousness, commonly referred to as "heat wind." There are typically two pathways: one is the presence of phlegm.[56p]
    In ordinary times, phlegm accumulates, filling the meridians. Once subjected to intense heat, phlegm obstructs the qi, leading to sudden drooling; this is the most severe manifestation of humid heat disease. It is advisable to first induce vomiting to expel the phlegm, then clear the heat, which is relatively easier to manage. In cases of deficiency, it is a deficiency of yang. When the yang qi is weak and inert over time, while yin cold has long been in effect, upon suddenly encountering intense heat, the evil harmonizes with this deficiency, which is how humid heat disease arises from deficiency and cold. It is best to use warming medicines that also clear the heat, which is the most difficult to address. Danxi stated: "Fire transforms gold into molten stone; what is there of cold?" This assertion seems overly one-sided and cannot avoid mistakes in two or three out of ten cases. Just as the snow atop Mount Emei has never melted, how can it be said that all temples are hot in summer due to this misconception? In the human body, if there is accumulated yin to the extent that even boiling water cannot warm it, how is this different? The Inner Canon states: "Seek what is lacking, and hold accountable what is deficient." This shows that we must not only seek and hold accountable for what is present and real. In my humble opinion, the Dahuang Long Wan can effectively revive those who have fainted due to heat stroke; isn’t that an example? There are also those with middle qi deficiency caused by emotional distress, where the qi is stagnant without phlegm; in such cases, Suhe Xiang Wan is advised for treatment.[57p]
    Scholar Xu said: This condition features a violent reversal of qi, yet it will quickly recover on its own. Even without medication, it can heal; however, afterward, it is advisable to clear away the summer heat. In summer, the yang of the human body leaks out through sweat, while the yin is consumed internally due to heat, resulting in a deficiency of both yin and yang. Zhongjing prohibited the use of sweating, purging, and warming needles in cases of draught illnesses because sweating harms the yang, purging injures the yin, and warming needles provoke internal heat, hence their ban. Instead, treatment focuses solely on using sweet and cold medicines that nourish fluids, protect the lungs, bolster the yang, and enrich the yin. The relationship between these factors is crucial, which is why I have highlighted this. The "Lingshu" states: When both yin and yang are deficient, replenishing yang exhausts yin, while draining yin leads to the loss of yang. This means that yang relies on yin as its home, so when replenishing yang, one must not harm yin; conversely, yin depends on yang as its root, so draining yin must not disrupt yang. When both yin and yang are deficient, one must be cautious about supplementation and draining; any efforts risk leaning too much toward one side, like an excess of yin or yang, resulting only in minute discrepancies. Therefore, excessive use of sweet and warm substances risks transgressing the caution against replenishing yang, while excessive bitter and cold substances could violate the caution against draining yin. However, when using a combination of sweet and cold—medications that do not favor either yin or yang—one can effectively clear and resolve summer heat. Thus, this has been the foundation for generations. In summer, sudden collapse and loss of consciousness is termed a summer wind.[58p]
    The heart's fire is exceedingly violent, and the heat of summer acts upon it, causing choking discomfort, obscuring one's awareness. However, there are other organs that may be weak and vulnerable, and when they are deeply affected by heat, they do not react as robustly as the heart. If the heat enters the liver, it causes dizziness and stubborn obstruction; if it enters the spleen, it leads to drowsiness and unawareness; if it enters the lungs, it results in panting and weakness; and if it enters the kidneys, it leads to excessive thirst.

    Both supplementation and cooling must be administered, but one must first ascertain which organ is involved, so that the treatment can be properly aligned. Those who labor in the heat at midday and are affected by the summer heat should be treated to cool and eliminate the heat toxins using methods such as Bai Hu Decoction, Yi Yuan Powder, Huang Lian Xiang Ru Drink, and San Huang Shi Gao Decoction, all of which can be employed.

    For those who reside in spacious buildings, are enveloped by cool breezes, eat raw and cold foods, and suppress their yang but fall ill from heat, all methods for treating summer heat and cooling should not be applied indiscriminately. If there is no sweating, one must still facilitate the surface to release the yang; if there are urgent symptoms of vomiting and diarrhea, one must harmonize and relieve internally; in severe cases, it is advisable to use warm medicines sparingly for further treatment. Thus, in treating summer heat-induced disorders characterized by vomiting and diarrhea, the primary focus should be on treating the summer heat; for those avoiding the heat who experience similar symptoms, the main approach should be to harmonize and warm the interior, making it essential to differentiate between the two.[59p]
    During the Yuanfeng era, the Harmony and Medicinal Bureau was established, gathering the experience of physicians, particularly detailed on the subject of heat stroke. In the summer months, due to heat-related conditions, extensive trials and observations were conducted. They employed a modified Xiao Banxia Fuling Decoction, which did not directly treat the heat but rather addressed the dampness; additionally, a formulation of Banxia and Fuling with a little licorice was created, named Xiaoshuwan, indicating that cooling down the symptoms was indeed about dispelling dampness, making this reasoning sound.

    Their Xiangru Drink primarily consisted of Xiangru, Cowpea, and Houpu; if heat was excessive, Cowpea was removed and Huanglian was added as the chief herb to treat heart fire; if dampness was excessive, Huanglian was removed and Fuling and licorice were added to combat spleen dampness. For the Squeeze Spleen Drink, since the spleen is affected by excessive dampness becoming congested, it was supplemented with Cowpea, Kudzu root, and licorice, along with Wumei, Sharen, and Caoguo to invigorate the spleen and eliminate its unwanted dampness; in severe cases, Dazun Powder and Laifu Dan were used to treat cases of heat stroke characterized by excessive diarrhea, aligning with the intent of squeezing the spleen.

    As for the Loquat Leaf Powder, it was used to address the stomach being overtaken by dampness, leading to turbidity. Hence, they included Xiangru, Loquat leaves, Clove, and Baimaoxiang for their aromatic qualities to soothe the stomach and eliminate any foul odor; in severe cases, they employed Cold Fragrance Drink to treat instances of heat stroke characterized by excessive vomiting, which also aligned with the principles of Loquat Leaf Powder.[60p]
    The physician, when treating conditions of heat, dampness, deficiency, cold, shallow, deep, slow, or rapid, should carefully consider and apply remedies; the benefits are extensive. Later scholars continued to build upon this by addressing deficiencies. The "Gui Ling Gan Lu Drink" from Hejian, along with Wu Ling and San Shi medications, aims to generate bodily fluids to help with stomach deficiency. Zi He’s "Gui Ling Gan Lu Powder" contains ginseng, kudzu root, licorice, patchouli, and amomum, which not only strengthens deficiencies but also eliminates turbidity; some use the "Shi Wei Xiang Rou Drink", enhancing the five flavors in the "Jufang" with added ginseng, astragalus, white atractylodes, dried tangerine peel, and papaya, to strengthen deficiencies and eliminate damp heat. Moreover, Dongyuan’s "Qing Shu Yi Qi Soup" and "Ren Shen Huang Qi Soup" also replenish the body and support immunity to eliminate damp heat, which is especially crucial for those who are internally weak and pale, regardless of whether they have suffered from heatstroke; these should be taken frequently. Heatstroke typically manifests as agitation and a feeling of heat and heaviness; Dongyuan devised a remedy called "Qing Zao Soup," modeled after Zhang Zhongjing’s "Zhu Ye Shi Gao Soup", focusing primarily on eliminating dampness. Dryness opposes dampness, and so the aim of dispelling dampness is paramount, demonstrating that Dongyuan possesses exceptional knowledge in this regard.[61p]
    Moreover, just as the Yiyuan powder expels dampness, adding cinnabar also removes heat; the Wuling powder expels dampness, while adding ginseng benefits deficiency and reducing cassia by adding cinnabar removes heat; in addition, Baihu decoction with ginseng benefits deficiency, and adding angelica wins against dampness. Combined with the "Jufang," it is quite comprehensive, yet it is still lacking in certain aspects. The observation of heatstroke resembles a stroke, and one cannot treat it through the channels of the wind. Although the "Baiyi Xuanfang" has the Dahuang Long Wan, it was not initially intended for heatstroke. I tentatively commend it, saying: for those who faint due to heat, administering it will provide relief, thus it can be used to address heatstroke to some extent. If a person has already depleted Yin and blood, and the heat poison penetrates deeply into the blood, advancing with this pill could certainly lead to peril. The "Liangfang" also has the Diyi powder to treat those who are in a stupor from heatstroke and are on the brink of death, but it employs common cooling blood medications to clear the heat-affected blood. There is nothing better than this. Later, having used it effectively multiple times, it was praised with the name "Pohuo powder." How insightful! For the celestial fire in the center of heaven flows like molten gold and shines like stone; this can extinguish it, further illustrating that the heatstroke is caused by excessive heart fire, boiling and depleting Yin blood. Other than clearing the heart and cooling the blood, there is nothing else that can be done. In reviewing various prescriptions and detailing them, more detail is added for clarity.[62p]
    On the Treatment of Summer Heat in the "Jinkui" with Two Formulas: Baihu Jiajinshen Decoction and Guati Decoction

    In the "Jinkui," there are two formulas for treating summer heat: one is Baihu Jiajinshen Decoction, which primarily addresses the heat caused by the intense summer. The excessive heat can disturb and injure the lung's metal element, depleting its fluids, and this formula is used to preserve the lung's metal element and its fluids. The Shengmai Powder by Sun Simiao and the Qingshu Yiqi Decoction by Li Dongyuan also have their roots in this. The other formula is Guati Decoction, which mainly treats dampness. During summer, the pervasive humidity can exacerbate heat, also injuring the lung's metal element, leading to excess moisture accumulating beneath the skin.[63p]
    The skin is the counterpart of the lungs, used to contain the moisture within the chest. When it is expelled or released, the lung qi is unblocked, and the moisture between the skin can descend. Why do later scholars only focus on Zhang Zhongjing's Wuling Powder? Take for example Hejian's Tongling Powder and Zihe's Guiling Ganlu Drink; they are clearly intended to guide dampness and alleviate summer heat. Whether extending the principles of the Guiti Decoction for treating upper burner damp-heat and clearing lung metal, there are indeed no other formulas. Thus, I specifically mention two formulas and discuss their meanings: when unseen heat injures lung metal, White Tiger Decoction with Ginseng is used to rescue it; when tangible dampness injures lung metal, Guiti Decoction is used to rescue it. Each has its own focus. These two formulas are not recorded in the chapter on cold-induced spasm due to damp heat in "Shanghan Lun" but appear again in the chapter on spasm due to damp heat in "Jinkui Yao Lue." Recognizing the subtle yet critical distinctions is essential.

    Regarding White Tiger Decoction with Ginseng, the text states: when there is internal heat in the Taiyang stage, this is termed "heat stroke." The person sweats and feels cold, has a fever and thirst; White Tiger Decoction with Ginseng is indicated. Indeed, those who sweat and feel cold in summer have a deficiency of the defensive qi. Fever and thirst indicate that lung metal has been dried out by the fire's control. The "Huangdi Neijing" says: when the heart transfers heat to the lungs, it causes dryness in the diaphragm. This state also leads to thirst. The heart fire affecting the lung metal is associated with the Taiyang pattern but differs from treating cold exposure in winter. Using this decoction to rescue lung metal is the fundamental principle.[64p]
    Gua Ti Decoction. This text states: In the case of a hot sensation in the middle (of the body), accompanied by bodily heat and heavy pain, along with a weak pulse, this results from summer's cold water damaging, causing water to flow within the skin. Gua Ti Decoction is indicated for this condition. Transform the powder into a decoction, removing red adzuki beans and sour jiaogulan, and solely using Gua Ti to prepare and take as a decoction. This will expel the water from the chest, and thus the water within the skin can also be eliminated. There is the meaning of discharge in "expel," leading to sweat as a result, and no longer will water seep between the skin. This corresponds to the "Neijing," where water fills sweat, leading to a lack of sweating. Zhang Zhongjing understood this concept, stating that middle heat also involves middle dampness, which has a relation. However, if water moves in the skin, why is the pulse seen as weak? It is because the middle heat pulse is originally weak, and dampness resides in the skin, internally connecting with the lungs, obstructing the flow of nutrients and defenses, causing the pulse to appear even weaker. The heat pulse is weak, pressing down reveals no strength; the damp pulse is weak, raising it is unbeneficial; thus, the pulse resulting from the combination of dampness and heat is also unbeneficial in both raising and pressing.[65p]
    To remove the water and promote circulation, the movement of lung energy is important. If the fingers are nimble and then become wet, it indicates a need to eliminate moisture. If pressing reveals strength, it suggests a need to relieve heat. Even a small object can have remarkable and swift effects.

    Moisture travels within the skin; during summer, it may come from excess cold water intake or from dousing with cold water after sweating, leading to moisture accumulation in the skin, obstructing outward flow of yang energy. This causes symptoms of heat and heaviness in the body. Using a gourd stem can drive away this moisture, allowing yang energy to circulate and relieving the condition.

    In general, consuming cold drinks injures the lungs, and the damage accumulates over time. This moisture mainly harms the skin associated with the lungs, so addressing it effectively requires only a small adjustment rather than relying heavily on red adzuki beans or sour jujube water. It is clear that prolonged exposure to cold, especially from wind, rain, and dew, can obstruct the upward flow of yang from the upper body, while moisture from the earth can further impact the lower body. Thus, treatments differ based on whether to enhance urination or not. Is there any need for further differentiation?[66p]
    Bian Mai (Differentiating Pulse)

    The pulse of heat injury, as stated in the "Inner Canon," is described as weak with a body heat resulting from heat injury. The "Jia Yi Jing" says that heat injures the qi without affecting the physical body, which is why the pulse is weak. However, the "Nan Jing" states that the pulse is floating, large, and scattered, indicating something yet to happen. Floating, large, and scattered pulses are fundamental to the heart's pulse, not indicative of illness. Zhongjing did not speak of this, but only addressed its unevenness, stating it is thin, wiry, and slow. The thin, wiry pulse corresponds to the response of qi injured by heat. The pulse flowing through the skin as water is said to be weak and shows the pulse retained by water and dampness, with yang qi not flowing. Generally, it is referred to as "weak." In detail, it is described as thin, wiry, slow, and weak. It is not the case that the floating, large pulse mixes into the weak pulse, which would be called the pulse of illness due to heat, raising concerns about the surrounding issues![67p]
    In treating heat stroke, if one does not distinguish between external causes and internal injuries, and only follows established formulas without considering activity and rest, the physician is at fault. In cases of cold disorders combined with heat, mistakenly using Yang Dan decoction leads to syncope. In cases of heat stroke with cold, mistakenly using Xiangru decoction results in throat obstruction. The later sages, by adding ginseng, astragalus, white atractylodes, dried tangerine peel, and papaya to Xiangru decoction to address internal injuries, indeed had good insights. However, those who do not differentiate between symptoms cause errors; can we stop here?

    In treating heat stroke, failing to address the dampness is a physician's error. Heat steams the dampness, leading to heat stroke; without dampness, it is merely dry heat, not heat stroke. Thus, overweight individuals with excess dampness frequently suffer from heat stroke, while thin individuals with excess fiery conditions frequently suffer from heat illnesses.

    When treating heat stroke, if the patient does not sweat, one must prioritize inducing sweating. If one only clears the interior without addressing the exterior, it is a physician's fault. Heat stroke should always result in profuse sweating; if there is no sweat, it is either due to water and dampness holding the person back or wind and cold obstructing. In such cases, one should first disperse the exterior pathogens, then induce sweating before clearing the interior.[68p]
    If one does not first resolve the external cause, then the cooling measures will not be effective enough and it may lead to conditions like intermittent fever and dysentery, causing endless troubles.

    In treating heatstroke, one must not recklessly administer warming tonics, as this causes the summer pathogen to penetrate deeply, aggravating the blood and becoming a fault of the physician.

    Heat harms the qi, leading to fatigue and shortness of breath that resembles deficiency. Thus, one should combine cooling measures for summer heat with those that boost qi. Those who do not understand may mistakenly apply warming tonics, allowing the summer pathogen to invade the blood, resulting in bleeding and dysentery, which may worsen in the harsh winter without relief. Therefore, mistaking heat for warmth in treatment carries endless harm.

    Damp warmth is a febrile illness resulting from the combination of summer heat and dampness. Pre-existing dampness can invite summer heat inside. When both legs feel cold, and there is abdominal fullness, dampness has revealed its cold nature under the influence of heat. If it enters the chest, it causes headaches and distress, leading to nonsensical speech; the summer heat combined with dampness exhibits its heat.

    At such times, one should only focus on resolving the combined evil of heat and dampness, thereby extinguishing its flames. It is better to induce sweating than to allow the two evils to merge into one. During sweating, one may be unable to speak, hear, or recognize distress, but their complexion becomes blue and their appearance changes, revealing the problem externally. If severe sweating occurs followed by critical condition, it is not a true or false comparison, but rather a matter of life and death for the patient in the hands of the physician.[69p]
    On Verification

    Heat illness originates from the heat of summer. However, there are those who suffer from heat stroke and those who become ill due to heat, resulting in different kinds of illnesses, all ultimately due to heat. Therefore, there are two kinds of illnesses: yin heat and yang heat, which must be treated differently, much like ice and fire. Those who seek to eliminate heat may inadvertently suffer from cold.

    If people fear heat and crave coolness without avoiding cold drafts, they may find themselves in deep halls or under shady trees, or during times when it’s both hot and cold, not being careful with their clothing. This can lead to cold pathogens invading the surface of the body, resulting in symptoms such as fever, headache, chills without sweating, stiff body, and aching limbs. This condition, caused by cold exposure in summer, is labeled as yin heat, corresponding to wind-cold disease. The main treatment should focus on warming and dispersing, using methods for treating wind-cold.

    Additionally, there are cases of negligence concerning diet, overindulging in raw and cold foods, leading to chills that harm the organs, resulting in symptoms such as vomiting, diarrhea, abdominal pain, etc. This is also due to heat leading to cold, but with cold pathogens present internally; therefore, treatment should primarily focus on warming the center, which also belongs to yin heat.

    Yang heat, on the other hand, refers to illnesses caused by excessive heat exposure.[70p]
    In the case of Zhongjing, it is referred to as "zhong re" (middle heat). Generally, during the peak of summer's intense heat, whether on long journeys or in the fields, when one does not shy away from labor, the excessive heat can injure the yin, leading to symptoms such as headache, irritability, high fever, intense thirst, profuse sweating, floating pulse, and possibly breathlessness or an inability to move. This is named "yang shu" (yang heat) because the heat occurs in the summer months. The treatment should focus on discerning the vacuity or solidity of the qi, and the mildness or severity of the fire, either supplementing or clearing to stabilize the qi. This is very different from the treatment of "yin shu" (yin heat). If one only considers the concept of heat and neglects to differentiate between exterior and interior, and does not understand yin and yang, it will lead to serious mistakes.

    Yin heat symptoms may occur either superficially or internally, mostly found in wealthy and comfortable individuals. This often arises from indulging in emotions without restraint, making them susceptible to wind and cold. Yang heat symptoms mostly afflict those engaged in hard labor, resulting from direct exposure to summer heat, often beyond their capacity to endure. However, the heat of summer can be avoided, and those who are cautious tend to evade it, leading to fewer cases. In contrast, cold can invade without notice, and since many are unaware, they may easily fall victim to it. Therefore, among those who suffer from summer heat, cases of yang heat are rare, while yin heat constitutes eight or nine out of ten.[71p]
    Nowadays, when people treat heatstroke, they merely observe symptoms like fever and headaches, and they must conclude that it is heatstroke, using only cooling methods, which shows a great lack of understanding.

    The illness caused by cold, although it originates from the same cold pathogen, is named differently. Similarly, although "heat injury" stems from the same heat pathogen, the diseases differ. The names for cold injuries differ: in winter’s cold, it is called true cold injury; in the warmth of spring, it is called warm disease; and in summer’s heat, it is termed heat disease. Warm disease and heat disease are simply different names for cold injury. The classic texts state: "In winter, one is injured by cold; in spring, one will surely fall ill with warmth." It is also said: "Any illness caused by cold that turns into warmth, if it occurs before the summer solstice, is warm disease; if after, it is heat disease." This is the distinction made.

    Heat injury also has differences: if one is impacted by heat and subsequently feels cold, that cold injures the body, thus it is cold injury. If one is affected by heat and then suffers from heat, that heat injures the qi, thus it is heat injury. This refers to injuries from within or affects from without, but there are simply different types of heat disease. The texts state: "If the qi is strong but the body is cold, it arises from cold injury; if the qi is weak but the body is hot, it arises from heat injury." This is the explanation.[72p]
    When the body's qi is abundant but the body is cold, it means the body is afflicted by cold pathogens while the qi remains intact; hence it is called "cold injury." When the qi is weak but the body is hot, it indicates overheating that injures the qi; thus it is termed "heat injury." Many people do not understand this point and mistakenly believe that cold injury must always involve a cold body, which is logically inconsistent and greatly misinterprets the texts.

    In the summer months when the heat is intense, one must ensure that the body has a slight sweat, as this is the most appropriate way to nourish the body during this season. If one insists on being completely dry without sweating, it tends to result in an excess of yin over yang, leading to various illnesses. Observing the classics, it is stated: "Heat should be accompanied by sweating; do not suppress it." This indicates that sweating during heat should not be hindered. It is also stated: "If sweating does not occur during summer heat, it will lead to autumn's wind and fever." This highlights that a lack of sweating during summer heat can be a disease. Thus, the appropriateness of sweating in the summer can be understood.

    There are eight signs of heatstroke: weak pulse, spontaneous sweating, body heat, coldness in the back, facial dirtiness, restlessness and thirst, slightly cold hands and feet, and a feeling of heaviness in the body. In treating these conditions, it is crucial to prioritize the regulation of the original qi, followed by clearing and benefiting.

    In cases of heatstroke resulting in death, one should not allow exposure to cold; doing so can be fatal. It is preferable to use warm substances to protect the abdomen and treat the condition slowly.[73p]
    Discussion on Treating Yin Heat Disorders

    In the hot months, if one is externally affected by wind and cold, it can lead to the suppression of Yang Qi by Yin evil, resulting in symptoms such as fever, headache, stiff and painful limbs, lack of sweating, aversion to cold, and a tense pulse. These are related to the category of cold damage, and the treatment should primarily focus on dispersing and resolving the condition, using formulas such as Xiao Chai Hu Tang or similar variations like Yi Er San Si Chai Hu Yin, adapting for cold-heat and deficiency-excess as appropriate.

    If the pulse is seen as weak and subtle, with a weak energy not suitable for sweating, one should focus on tonifying the central Qi to gradually restore the primal energy, allowing the cold evil to disperse without direct attack on it. One may also use Bu Zhong Yi Qi Tang for this. If the exterior is affected by evil while there is intense internal heat, or severe Yangming heat, then formulas such as Chai Hu Bai Hu Jian should be employed.

    If the cold evil has not yet resolved on the surface, with the six pulses feeling weak and tight, a cold back, aversion to cold, or symptoms like vomiting and diarrhea without internal heat, this indicates that true Yin is concealed within, and the evil is not easily resolved. Even during the hot months, immediate gentle warming of the center is warranted; formulas like Li Yin Jian, Li Zhong Tang, Da Wen Zhong Yin, and Ma Gui Yin should be used promptly, without hesitation or delay.[74p]
    If evil excesses from the outside manifest, yet the interior is not greatly deficient, one may use the Five Accumulations Powder. For the above symptoms that cannot all be addressed, one should observe and treat according to the principles of the harmful cold category.

    As for internal injuries caused by cold, resulting in stomach qi depletion and symptoms like abdominal pain, diarrhea, and vomiting, the primary treatment should involve warming the middle and dispersing cold. If one has just been exposed to cold evil, with stagnation not yet relieved and the spleen’s qi not deficient, one should primarily use suppressive and supportive decoctions like the Suppressive Brew and the Five Virtues Pills. If the stomach qi is slightly deficient, it is advisable to supplement with the Guanyuan Decoction and the Five Virtues Pills. If the stomach qi is severely deficient, one should focus on the Warming Stomach Drink and the Regulate the Middle Decoction. If vomiting and diarrhea are severe, with both the spleen and kidneys injured, resulting in pain radiating to the lower abdomen or even dysentery, the Warming Stomach Guanyuan Decoction, the Regulate the Yin Decoction, or variations like the Nine Qi Pill should be used.

    If there is cold evil in the exterior, causing internal cold injuries, leading to illness both in the exterior and interior, it is necessary to treat both aspects, using the Harmony Stomach Drink with Bupleurum or a warm stomach drink with Bupleurum, or newly formulated Bupleurum drinks, while carefully examining the deficiency or excess. If ancient formulas like the Great Harmony Powder are employed as a general treatment for warming the middle, they are certainly not adequate alone.[75p]
    Discussion on Treating Summer Heat

    Severe heat can harm a person, deriving from heat symptoms; however, within Yang, there are also Yin and Yang, which must be distinguished. Generally, those suffering from summer heat tend to have a deficiency of Qi, as fire can undermine metal, and heat damages Qi. It is essential to clear heat and to replenish deficiency. For those who are predominantly Yang within Yang, they should also be treated with clearing methods. Symptoms like body heat, headache, irritability, intense thirst, profuse sweating, and a rapid, slippery pulse, accompanied by a preference for cold water, dry stools, and painful, red urine, all indicate Yang symptoms. If the Qi is not significantly deficient but there are just fiery symptoms, one should consider White Tiger Decoction or Yiyuan Powder. If the heat is very intense, Jade Spring Powder would be even better. If sweating occurs with a weak pulse, thirst is present, and there is fire with scant Qi, White Tiger Decoction with Ginseng or Zhang Jing's Bamboo Leaf and Gypsum Decoction, or drinks like those in "Xuanning" with cinnamon, Poria, and sweet dew should be considered. If dizziness and shortness of breath occur, though thirst is present and fire isn’t too severe, Sheng Mai Powder should be used. The methods mentioned above for treating Yang within Yang are all from ancient wisdom. However, if there is intense heat and thirst, yet a weak and powerless pulse, or if pressing down produces no response, along with a feeling of weakness and rapid breathing, this indicates a deficiency of Spleen and Stomach Qi and insufficient original Yang. This is a false fire condition; mistakenly using White Tiger or similar formulas could lead to a critical situation.[76p]
    On Treating Hidden Yin in Heat Stroke

    In cases of heatstroke, people usually recognize it as a manifestation of Yang, but they fail to understand that there is Yin within Yang. When there is external heat afflicting the body along with internal heat, both exterior and interior are hot, which is indeed a Yang condition, treated appropriately with clearing and tonifying methods as described earlier. However, if there is no internal heat originally, but injury to Qi due to heat, resulting in Qi deficiency within, then there are signs of hidden Yin. Thus, in treating symptoms of heatstroke, it is most important to distinguish between Yin and Yang, deficiency and excess. If the pulse is weak and without strength, or if there are symptoms like aversion to cold, chills in the back, nausea and vomiting, abdominal pain and diarrhea, or if the limbs and the tips of the nose feel slightly cold, or a disinclination for cool tea or cold water, or shortness of breath with weakness upon movement, all these are signs of Yin within Yang. In such cases, the best approach is to focus on preserving the vital energy, specifically using Shenqi Decoction slowly with it. If there are also mild nausea and chills, ginger should be added in equal measure with ginseng. In more severe cases, one should apply nourishing decoctions, middle-regulating decoctions, Five Gentleman Decoction, Five Fortune Drink, or Decoctions for regulating Yin, all of which should be used as appropriate.[77p]
    If the excess of cold and emptiness is severe, then one must rely on suitable remedies regardless of the time. Both cinnamon and goji berries are essential. It is crucial not to use cold and cooling agents just because of the heat, which could further harm the yang energy and lead to unpredictable consequences. In the summer months, if one is overly fatigued during intense heat and suffers from heatstroke, their fatigue has already harmed the spleen, and the heat further damages the energy. This is fundamentally a case of internal injury with great emptiness, so one should prioritize nourishment and then check for the presence of fire or evil, treating accordingly as previously mentioned.

    In summer, illnesses caused by heat can occur, and if a physician unknowingly uses cold remedies without recognizing hidden yin, it may lead to vomiting, diarrhea, abdominal pain, or external heat with internal cold, along with irritability and thirst. These symptoms can resemble a cold illness, but if one observes a weak pulse and spiritual fatigue, it indicates a condition of excessive yin countering yang; hence, warming remedies should be promptly applied to rescue the internal state.

    In summer, if heat has been harmed and cold has likewise harmed the body, in cases of external heat with internal cold, the focus should be primarily on addressing the internal cold. If there is stagnation, one should clear the obstruction; if there isn’t, one should augment the energy. Warming the center and regulating the spleen will restore the spleen energy, allowing the heat to dissipate completely.[78p]
    On the Hidden Yin in Summer Months

    The hidden Yin during the summer months is a principle of the natural ebb and flow of Yin and Yang in the world. However, Danxi has specifically addressed this issue yet has interpreted it contrary to its true meaning, leading people to doubt its essence. What does he mean? He discusses that humans share a connection with Heaven and Earth. In the first month (Zi), Yang is born, and it begins to move; in the third month (Yin), three Yangs are born, and Yang first emerges from the ground. This represents the rising energy. In the sixth month (Si), six Yangs are born, and Yang fully emerges above, signifying the floating energy. The human abdomen corresponds to the Earth's energy and at this time, it floats at the surface of the skin, dispersing into the dermis, rendering the abdomen empty.

    People say that the hidden Yin is within during the summer months, and this notion of Yin implies emptiness. If one interprets it merely as coldness, it leads to significant misunderstandings. Moreover, during this period, Yang is abundant above ground, burning and scorching, flowing like molten gold and dazzling like stone; how can there be anything cold or Yin? If one carelessly introduces warmth during the summer, can they avoid the troubles of an imbalance between reality and illusion? Danxi's mention of emptiness is indeed correct. However, treating coldness as a misconception while also prohibiting warmth in summer is something I cannot accept.[79p]
    How can this be understood? The way of heaven and earth relies on yin and yang. The transformations of yin and yang are based on growth and decline. Thus, when one comes, another departs; when one rises, another falls, and the mechanisms of creation are mutually concealed for utility. The classics say: Yin governs cold, and yang governs heat. It also states: Qi that is abundant is hot, while qi that is deficient is cold. This is the fundamental nature of yin and yang. Now, since it is said that in summer the yang floats outside, then the yin must be hidden within. When yin is strong, yang is weak; how can it not be cold? When yang floats outside, qi is deficient inside; how can it not be cold? This is a principle that is inherently unchanging. However, what is even more evident can be seen in the water of wells and springs: during the harsh cold of three winters, the well water is warm; during the scorching heat of midsummer, the spring water is cold. Is this not a clear example of external cold and internal heat, or external heat and internal cold? This holds true year after year and represents the constant seasons of qi. Beyond the primary qi, there are also foreign qi, with heaven set in five phases and earth equipped with six, where cold and warmth alternate and qi changes correspondingly.[80p]
    Just as the record of the cold and clear during the伏明 period has been frequently noted; the record of wind and cold during the卑监 period has also risen; the record of the sun's energy in conjunction with the control of yin and its transformation during the坚成 period; and the record of cold influencing the transformation of things during the流衍 period, when heaven and earth become solemn and still; the sun governs heaven, and cold air descends, bringing forth cold and clear times; the moon governs heaven, hiding the yin within the earth, leading to great cold, etc. This means that regardless of winter or summer, there exist unseasonal energies that provoke ailments among the people. Moreover, should we claim that due to the fire of summer months, there is no cold, and thus prohibit the use of warmth? The principle of伏阴 (hidden yin) essentially refers to the interplay of yin and yang in relation to cold and heat; if one were to merely treat the word "cold" as void, then in summer, hidden yin would imply many void syndromes, while in winter, with hidden yang, there would be none remaining—could it be so? Further, if in summer, warmth should be prohibited, then in winter, cold should equally be banned—this is simply self-evident.

    Now, observing the ailments across the four seasons, it is clear that in midsummer there are often many cases of vomiting and diarrhea, while in the depths of winter, rashes and sores are particularly evident. Such conditions—are they not due to an excess of internal heat in winter and an abundance of middle cold in summer? In summary, in summer there are heat syndromes and also cold syndromes; in winter there are excess syndromes and also void syndromes. Although treatment should vary based on the season and symptoms, it is critical to adapt according to the illnesses encountered. However, the principle of伏阴 in summer truly reflects the synchrony of heaven and humanity, being the original mechanism of disease that must not be overlooked.[81p]
    If we consider the arguments of Danxi, they contradict the principles of reasoning; what relevance do they have to practice? Even without these arguments, what harm is there? Recently, I saw Xu Donggao also discussing Danxi’s ideas, saying: “In summer, there is no cold; people fail to notice this and use warmth, which leads to common errors.” If it is said that in summer one should take warming foods, then in winter one should take cooling foods. In that case, Mencius’s advice to drink soup in winter and water in summer would also be untrustworthy, wouldn't it? Alas! These words reflect a borrowing from a broader concept, and do not fundamentally engage in the analysis of yin and yang. Yet, Xu quotes them as evidence, without contemplating the texts and their meanings, perhaps contradicting themselves!

    The "Inner Canon" states: "There is yin within yang, and yang within yin." It says: "Extreme cold generates heat, and extreme heat generates cold." It declares: "Heavy yin must produce yang, and heavy yang must produce yin." It says: "Under the correspondence of fire, water vapor is sustained; under the sovereign fire, yin essence is sustained." These all express the interplay of yin and yang, the internal and external dynamics of complementing and opposing forces, thus responding to the heavenly yin and yang. Moreover, similarly in the "I Ching," within the duality of yin and yang, there must be both; with dualities leading to four images, within yin and yang further lies the interplay of yin and yang.

    In the context of the hexagram Tai, it speaks of inner yang and outer yin; the path of the noble person flourishes, while the path of the petty person declines. In the context of the hexagram Pi, it speaks of inner yin and outer yang; in this case, the path of the petty person flourishes, while the path of the noble person declines. From this perspective, the arguments of Danxi and the citations of Donggao are unconvincing to me, which is why I continue this discussion.[82p]
    On Xiangru Drink

    Xiangru drink is a herbal remedy commonly used in summer, often seen among wealthy families who prepare it abundantly, taken regularly by both young and old to prevent heat illness. However, few know who it truly benefits and who it does not. If misused, it certainly leads to illness. Why is this? Xiangru is aromatic and has a sinking, cold nature. Its aroma allows it to penetrate and reach both the upper and lower parts of the body, helping to dispel the damp heat of summer; its coldness enables it to quench thirst, alleviate restlessness, and clear the excessive fire evil.

    However, it must be noted that it is suitable only for those with a strong yang organ, those who actually have fire evil, and those with robust spleen and stomach energy who tolerate excess warmth. Only they can withstand it, and they depend on its cooling effects, which are not without benefits. If taken by those whose energy is already deficient, it will likely deplete their energy further; if taken by those without real fire, it will undermine their yang. Generally, individuals who are naturally weak, aged, in poor health, or with inadequate diets are unaware of the risks and often misuse it. They typically exacerbate the hidden yin, weaken their stomach energy, and are susceptible to symptoms like vomiting, diarrhea, abdominal pain, and severe cold conditions. Adding Huanglian further increases the coldness, while Houpo and Peiling, which break up qi, are not advisable. Users must be cautious![83p]
    Fang

    Qing Zhong Tang 【from the "Zhong Cang Jing"】
    For treating heat stroke and thirst.
    1 liang of licorice, honey roasted until brittle
    ½ liang of dried ginger, wrapped in wet paper and baked
    2 liang of dried tangerine peel
    Grind the above into powder; take 2 qian with 1 cup of water, decoct until reduced to 80%, and consume warm or cool as a soup, adjusting with water as needed.

    Leng Xiang Yin Zi 【from the "Ji Sheng Fang"】
    For treating older individuals or those who have been weak and experience heat exhaustion, excessive thirst, nausea, and fatigue, especially when cooling medicines are ineffective.
    3 liang of grass fruit seeds
    Prepared aconite, removing skin and navel
    1 liang of tangerine peel
    ½ liang of licorice
    Chop the above; take 1 liang with 2 bowls of water and 10 slices of fresh ginger, decoct until reduced to half a bowl, remove the dregs, cool, and consume as needed without time restrictions.

    Wu Ling San 【Zhongjing】
    For treating heat.
    1.5 liang each of stir-fried white atractylodes, polyporus, and poria
    1 liang of cinnamon
    2.5 liang of water plantain
    Add cinnabar to the above, known as Cinnabar Wu Ling San.

    Huang Lian Jie Du Tang 【from the "Dan Xi Xin Fa"】
    For treating heat stroke.
    Equal parts of coptis, phellodendron, scutellaria, and gardenia.[84p]
    Huanglian Xiangru Decoction: Treats summer heat causing thirst, dry mouth, and either vomiting or diarrhea.
    Ingredients: 4 taels of Huanglian, 1 pound of Xiangru, 0.5 pound of thick-skinned dried orange peel, ginger juice roasted until cooked, 0.5 pound of slightly fried white hyacinth beans.
    Preparation: Grind the ingredients, take 5 qian of the mixture, add 2 bowls of water and a small amount of wine, simmer until 1 bowl remains, let cool; can be taken at any time.

    Tangerine Peel Decoction: Treats heat stroke with phlegm and chills.
    Ingredients: 2 taels of tangerine peel without the white part, 1 tael of fresh ginger, 5 jujubes pitted, 0.5 tael of roasted licorice, 1 qian of ginseng, 0.5 sheng of bamboo shavings.
    Preparation: Separate into 5 doses, each with 1.2 bowls of water, boil down to 0.8 bowls, strain and serve hot.

    Xiaoshu Pills: Treats headache, nausea, irritability, thirst, and cholera.
    Ingredients: 4 taels each of mung bean powder and gypsum, 1 tael each of alum, calcined sulfur.
    Preparation: Soak and steam into small ball-sized pills, coat with cinnabar; use ginger juice and fresh collected water to dissolve and take.

    Suoping Drink: Clears summer heat and alleviates thirst.
    Ingredients: 4 taels each of Suanshen, dried plum flesh, stewed grass fruit, and roasted licorice; 2 taels each of dried kudzu and peeled roasted white hyacinth beans.
    Preparation: Each dose is 4 qian with 1 bowl of water, simmer to 0.7 bowls, let cool to alleviate annoyance, can be taken hot or warm as desired, or with well-cooked water for maximum effect.[85p]
    Xiangru Chuo Drink
    Relieves summer heat and stops cholera.
    Xiangru and Houpu Ginger, each 6 grams; Bai Fuling 4.5 grams; Gan Cao 1.5 grams; Chen Pi and Liang Jiang, each 3 grams.
    Mix these into one dose, with 480 ml of water, add a little salt, and decoct until 240 ml remains. Take at any time.

    Bamboo Leaf and Gypsum Decoction
    Treats both external and internal heat from summer heat, with irritability and great thirst.
    Gypsum 15 grams; Mai Men Dong and Ren Shen, each 6 grams; Gan Cao 4.5 grams; wash Ban Xia with water seven times, 4.5 grams.
    Mix these into one dose, with 480 ml of water, add 5 slices of fresh ginger, 14 pieces of green bamboo leaves, a pinch of japonica rice, and decoct until 240 ml remains. Take at any time.

    Imperial Love Ginger and Su Pills
    Treats heat stroke with nausea, headaches, irritability; generates fluids and stops thirst.
    Zi Su Ye, 30 grams; Wu Mei flesh, roasted, 60 grams; Gan Cao, raw, 1.5 grams; Gui Xin (cinnamon heart) stripped of coarse skin; Xiangru, each 15 grams; fresh ginger, chopped and dried, 1 gram; dried papaya, 60 grams, peel and chop as fresh before drying.
    Grind these into a fine powder, mix with honey to form pills the size of cherries. Each dose: take one pill, chew thoroughly, and swallow with warm water.[86p]
    Shuipiao Pill: Treats heatstroke, alleviates thirst.
    Four ounces of black plum flesh, one ounce each of licorice, green salt, dried papaya, white poria, and sandalwood, and two and a half qian of musk. The musk should be ground separately; the rest should be made into a fine powder and formed into pills using honey, making thirty pills for every two ounces. Take one pill at a time, dissolve in the mouth or chew it down with fresh water or warm water, without concern for timing. One variant includes ginseng.

    Pohuo San (also known as Dihu San): Treats heat injury, restlessness, thirst, bitter mouth, dry tongue, headache, nausea, loss of appetite, confusion, and extreme lethargy.
    Equal parts of green rind (from the orange), red peony root, yellow gentian (with hairs removed), and elder root. Grind into a fine powder. Take three qian mixed with cold water. If there is retained heat with erratic circulation of qi and blood, add equal parts of licorice. Another variant mixes it with thin gruel; if that’s unavailable, fresh water can be used. For blood dysentery, boil one cup of water for consumption.

    Dahuang Long Pill (from "Baiyi Xuanfang"): Treats heatstroke with body heat and headache, resembling cold in the spleen, perhaps restlessness and thirst, vomiting, and lack of appetite. One ounce each of sulfur and saltpetre, half an ounce each of white alum, realgar, and talcum powder, and four ounces of wheat flour. Grind these five ingredients to a powder, mix with flour, and form into pills the size of a walnut. Take thirty pills with fresh spring water. It is said that for severe heatstroke leading to faintness, it can resuscitate immediately.[87p]
    Sandalwood Pills: Relieve heat and toxins.
    One qian of sandalwood, two and a half qian of peeled apricot kernels, three liang of black plum flesh, two liang of perilla leaves, three and a half qian of fennel, two qian of all-purpose pills, one and a half liang of licorice. Grind the above into a fine powder, mix with honey to form pills the size of a marble. Chew and dissolve from time to time.

    Five Saints Decoction: Treats summer sickness with vomiting and nausea. In summer, if there is a lack of appetite, fatigue, low energy, dizziness, headaches, nausea, and excessive sleep, the following ingredients are used:
    Guan Zhong, Yellow Coptis (removing the beard), roasted licorice, Wu Zhu Yu (washed seven times), and peeled white poria, each at half a liang. Chew and divide into two doses; each time take one and a half bowls of water, boil down to one bowl, strain and cool. At noon, first take one fragrant, ripe sweet melon, cut off the skin and make twelve slices. Chew one slice of melon, take one or two sips of the decoction, and then chew the next slice, continuing until all the melon slices are consumed and the medicinal liquid is depleted, ensuring not to harm the spleen and stomach or disturb the internal organs. This should be taken when feeling greatly troubled.

    Wax Plum Pills: Relieve heat, quench thirst, and generate fluids.
    One liang each of wax plum flower powder, dried ginger powder, loquat leaf powder, all-purpose pill powder, and black plum flesh powder; five liang of wax. First, melt the wax, then add two liang of honey mixed with the powdered ingredients, knead two or three hundred times to form pills the size of a chicken's head. In summer, for long journeys, dissolve one pill daily.[88p]
    Guangshun San - For treating heatstroke and thirst.

    Ingredients:

    • 3.5 taels of licorice,
    • 1 tael of roasted cinnamon bark (with coarse skin removed),
    • 1 tael of dry-fried ginger,
    • 0.5 tael of perilla leaves,
    • 1.5 taels of dried black plum flesh.

    Grind all into a fine powder. Take 2 qian each time, mixed with cold water or boiling water.

    Chaihu Decoction
    This is used for cold dispersal. During hot months, external wind-cold may impede yang energy, leading to symptoms such as fever, headache, body aches, lack of sweating, chilliness, and tight pulse. This belongs to the category of cold damage and is treated primarily with dispersing methods. Suitable prescriptions include Chaihu Decoction, Xiao Chaihu Decoction, or similar variations, adjusted according to the cold and heat, deficiency and excess of the condition.

    If there is external pathogenic influence and internal heat, cooling dispersal is necessary.

    Ingredients:

    • 1-2 qian of Chaihu,
    • 2 qian of Shaoyao,
    • 1.5 qian each of Huangqin, Chenpi (with white pith retained), and Shengdi,
    • 0.8 qian of licorice.

    Add 1.5 sheng of water and simmer down to 7-8 parts; take warm.

    For severe internal heat, add 1-2 qian of Lianqiao as needed. If the external pathogen is more severe, add 1 qian of Fangfeng as an adjunct.

    If the pathogen is constrained in the chest, leading to fullness and discomfort, remove Shengdi and add 1-2 qian of Zhishi. If heat is at Yangming and accompanies thirst, add 1-2 qian of Tianhuafen or Gegeng. If heat is very intense, add Zhimu or Shigao as well.[89p]
    Er Chaihu Decoction: This formula is for the condition of excess fire, stemming from warmth dispersing. It is used to treat those who, during the hot months, are affected by external wind and cold, particularly individuals whose vital energy is robust, and whose internal organs are generally balanced. For those without fire, it is unwise to recklessly use cooling medicines, as this can lead to stagnation of cold, which can cause significant harm; hence, this formula is prescribed.

    Ingredients:

    • Houpu (Magnolia Officinalis) and Chenpi (Dried Tangerine Peel), each 1.5 qian
    • Bei Xixin (Asarum Canada), 1-2 qian
    • Chaihu (Bupleurum), 1.5 to 2-3 qian
    • Licorice, 0.8 qian
    • Banxia (Pinellia), 2 qian
    • Fresh ginger slices, 3-5-7 slices
    • Water, 1.5 sheng, simmer until reduced by 7-8 parts, and take warm.

    If there is excess dampness, add Cangzhhu (Atractylodes).[90p]
    Three Chaihu Decoction: The number three corresponds to the wood element, relating to the liver and blood. This treats exterior wind-cold in the hot months for individuals who traditionally have insufficient yin, or who may have little liver blood, thus can both nourish and disperse. However, for those with weak blood energy that cannot be expressed externally, this prescription should be prioritized.

    Ingredients: 2-3 qian of Chaihu, 1 qian each of roasted licorice and aged tangerine peel (white part only), 1.5 qian of peony root, 3-5 slices of fresh ginger, 2 qian of angelica, and for those with loose stools, replace with cooked rehmannia. Use 1.5 sheng of water, decoct until reduced by 7-8 parts, and take warm.

    Four Chaihu Decoction: The number four corresponds to the metal element, relating to the qi aspect. This is for individuals with insufficient primal qi, or those who endure hunger and fatigue, experiencing exterior wind-cold during the hot months, or presenting with tight, rapid, and thin pulses, unable to withstand pathogenic factors. This prescription must bolster primal qi, while also dispersing, to protect overall health. It should be prioritized to prevent preceding loss of primal qi by solely focusing on dispersing pathogens.

    Ingredients: 1-2-3 qian of Chaihu, 1 qian of roasted licorice, 3-5-7 slices of fresh ginger, 2-3 qian of angelica (use less if there are loose stools), and 2-3 qian or 5-7 qian of ginseng. Use 2 sheng of water, decoct until reduced by 7-8 parts, and take warm.[91p]
    Wuchaihu Decoction: The number five corresponds to the earth, and it relates to the spleen and stomach. The spleen is the foundation of the five organs; whenever there is insufficient central qi and external pathogens do not disperse, this remedy is indispensable. This decoction complements the Four Chaihu Decoction, but while the latter primarily regulates the qi division, this one also nourishes blood and qi to drive away cold pathogens, making it especially pertinent for seasonal use. Its divine effectiveness is beyond full description.

    Ingredients: Chaihu (3-9 grams), stir-fried Baizhu (6-9 grams), Donggui (6-9 grams), Shudihuang (9-15 grams), Shaoyao (1.5 grams), stir-fried Gancao (3 grams), Chenpi (to taste, optional).

    Water: 1.5 liters, decoct to 750 ml, take warm after meals.

    Zheng Chaihu Decoction: In the hot months, if one contracts wind-cold symptoms such as headaches and body pains, but blood and qi are balanced, it is advisable to disperse them peacefully. This formula is for that purpose.

    Ingredients: Chaihu (3-9 grams), Chenpi (4.5 grams), Shaoyao (6 grams), Fangfeng (3 grams), Gan Cao (3 grams), fresh ginger (3-5 slices).

    Water: 1.5 liters, decoct to 600-750 ml, take warm.[92p]
    Chaihu Baihu Decoction: Used for conditions of summer heat during the yangming season, where the exterior caused by pathogens is unresolved, among other symptoms. Ingredients: Chaihu, gypsum, huangqin, and maidong, each two qian; fine licorice, seven fen; one and a half sheng of water, add twenty pieces of bamboo leaves, decoct and take.

    Yuqian Powder: Also known as Liu Yi Ganlu Powder, treats inner heat of the yangming during summer, symptoms include thirst, headache, constipation, and epidemic yellow spots, modified from Yiyuan Powder. Ingredients: Six liang of gypsum (raw), one liang of powdered licorice. Grind into a very fine powder; take one to three qian with freshly drawn water, hot soup, or ginseng soup. Adding three qian of cinnabar to this formula is also excellent.

    Li Yin Decoction: A modified version of the Li Zhong Decoction. For symptoms of spleen and kidney deficiency, those requiring dryness should use formulas like Li Zhong or Liu Jun; those needing warmth and moisture should use Li Yin or Da Ying types. To know how to adjust tonics, one must first observe this. This formula is commonly used to treat internal hidden cold during summer, where pathogens are difficult to resolve, especially in cases of heat symptoms with weak pulse; attacking with cold can be fatal. It is essential to use this decoction promptly. Ingredients: Shud di, three, five, or seven qian or one or two liang; danggui, two, three, five, or seven qian; roasted licorice, one or two qian; dry ginger, stir-fried to yellow, one, two, or three qian; optionally add cinnamon, one or two qian. Use two sheng of water, decoct to seven or eight parts, and take hot. If aconite is added, it is called Aconite Li Yin Decoction; if ginseng is added, it is known as Liuwei Huayang Drink, treating symptoms of declining life gate fire and absence of yang within yin.[93p]

      Xiang Ge Decoction: For symptoms of prior illness. Ingredients: 1.5 qian of Xiangru, 0.7 qian each of Houpu, Bian Dou, Huanglian; 0.3 qian of Shengma; 2 qian each of Genggen, Shaoyao; 1 qian of roasted Gancao. [Prepare by] chewing, adding ginger, and decocting.

    Youxing Pills: For summer thirst, to prevent drinking cold water causing abdominal distension, and to stop vomiting and diarrhea. Ingredients: Baicao Decoction, Maidong, Wumei, Genggen, Renshen, Gancao. Grind into fine powder, form into pills with honey, and dissolve one pill.

    Ginger-Houpo Decoction: For treating summer and autumn heat, overindulgence in cold substances or tea damaging the interior, excessive exposure to cool breezes harming the exterior, leading to chills and fever, chest fullness and oppression, loss of appetite, or vomiting and diarrhea; this indicates both internal and external cold. Ingredients: 1 qian each of dried ginger, Houpo, Renshen, Baizhu, and Bai Fuling; 0.5 qian each of Chenpi, Zhishi, Qianghuo, and roasted Gancao. [Prepare by] using two zhans of water, three slices of ginger, and decocting.

    White Tiger Decoction with Ginseng: For treating heat stroke. Ingredients: 6 liang of Zhi Mu, 1 jin of Gypsum, 2 liang of Gancao, 6 he of glutinous rice; this is the White Tiger Decoction, with 6 qian of Ginseng added. Use 10 dou of water, cook the rice until soft to form the decoction, strain, and take one sheng warm, three times a day.[95p]
    White Tiger and Cang Zhu Decoction
    For those going inside, add two taels of Cang Zhu. Increase water and divide into four doses.

    Xiao Shu Pill [from "He Ji"]
    Treats summer heat leading to excessive drinking, with spleen and stomach disorders. Ingredients: one jin of Ban Xia, five sheng of vinegar boiled down, half a jin each of fresh Licorice and Fu Ling. Grind the above into a fine powder, mix with ginger juice to form pills the size of a tung seed. Take fifty pills each time, without regard to timing, and swallow with hot soup. For heat stroke, this remedy works immediately; for summer heat causing fever and headache, it is particularly effective. Regular use in summer stops thirst and promotes urination; even with much water intake, it is harmless and more effective than other summer remedies. If phlegm or fluids stagnate, use ginger soup. After the beginning of summer, this medicine cannot be missed.

    Yi Yuan Powder also known as Tian Shui Powder, treats cold damage with both exterior and interior heat, thirst, dry mouth, urinary obstruction, and symptoms of cholera including vomiting and diarrhea. Ingredients: six taels of Ku Fu Hua and six taels of white slippery stone, ground to a fine powder. Take three qian each time, mixed with white soup or fresh water. Adding three qian of cinnabar makes it Yi Yuan Powder; adding a bit of mint changes it to Ji Su Powder; adding a bit of indigo creates Bi Yu Powder, treating the same conditions but designed to avoid worldly scorn.[96p]
    Ten-Flavor Xiangru Drink

    For relieving summer heat and harmonizing the spleen and stomach:

    • 1 tael of Xiangru
    • 1 tael of Ginseng
    • 1 tael of Chenpi (dried tangerine peel, white part removed)
    • 1 tael of Baizhu (white atractylodes)

    • 1 tael of Huangqi (astragalus)
    • 1 tael of Mugua (papaya)
    • 1 tael of Houpo (magnolia bark) fried with ginger juice until black
    • 0.5 tael of fried licorice

    Grind these into a fine powder. Each dose is 2 qian, taken at any time, mixed with hot soup or cold water.

    Huangqi and Ginseng Decoction

    For the above symptoms:

    • 1 qian of Huangqi, doubled if there is excessive sweating
    • 0.5 qian each of Chenpi, Ginseng, and Baizhu
    • 0.5 qian of Cangzhu (Atractylodes)
    • Doubled if there is no sweating
    • 0.2 qian each of fried Licorice, Dong Quai (Angelica) washed in wine, and Mai Men Dong (Ophiopogon)
    • 0.3 qian each of Huangbo washed in wine and Shenqu (fermented wheat)
    • 0.6 qian of Shengma (cimicifuga)
    • 9 pieces of Wuweizi (Schisandra)

    Use 2 cups of water, reduce to 1 cup, strain, and take hot, far from meals or on an empty stomach. Avoid wine, moist foods, and excessive cool items.

    Gua Di Decoction

    For treating solar heat sickness, with a hot body, heavy pain, and weak pulse:

    • 27 pieces of Gua Di (melon seeds)[97p]
      Here is the translated text:

    Right: Use one liter of water, boil to obtain five cups, remove the residue and take it immediately.
    Clear Dry Decoction 【Eastern Garden】 Treats the preceding symptoms.
    Cooked Astragalus, one and a half qian; Coptis, Atractylodes, and White Atractylodes (fried), each one qian; Schisandra, nine seeds; Ginseng, White Poria, and Cimicifuga, each three fen; Angelica, one qian and two fen; Dried Tangerine Peel and Alisma, each five fen; Bupleurum, Ophiopogon, raw Rehmannia, and Fried Fermented Rice, and Polyporus, each two fen; Hydrangea wine treated, and Licorice, both two fen.
    Take half a tael each time, with two cups of water, decoct to one cup, remove the residue, slightly warm, and take on an empty stomach.
    Water Kettle Lu Pills Treats heat stroke poison, relieves thirst.
    Chuan Baiyao decoction three liang; Ginseng two qian; Ophiopogon, Black Plum flesh, White Plum flesh, Dried Kudzu, and Licorice each half a liang. Grind into fine powder and form into pills the size of a chicken head. Take one pill for summer outings, sufficient for one day.
    Xiangru Pills 【“Harmonizing Recipe”】 Treats both adults and children suffering from summer heat and fever, thirst and malaise, dizziness, chest fullness, nausea, bitter mouth, dry tongue, fatigue, lack of appetite, or causing cholera, vomiting, diarrhea, or cramping. Xiangru one liang; Perilla, Papaya, Patchouli, and Poria each five qian; Licorice (roasted to red color), Sandalwood, and Cloves each two and a half qian. Grind into fine powder, mix with honey to form pills, making thirty pills from each two. Take one to two pills, chew well, and swallow with warm water.[98p]
    Shengmai San (Formula for Reviving the Pulse)
    Used to treat pre-symptoms.
    2 qian of Ophiopogon, 1 qian each of Ginseng and Schisandra.
    Boil in water and use as a tea substitute in summer.
    Optionally, add 1 qian each of Astragalus and Licorice, or add 2 fen of Coptis, to invigorate the energy and strength.

    Shenggui Yiyuan Tang (Ginseng and Rehmannia Decoction)
    Used to treat summer illnesses, symptoms include dizziness, blurry vision, tired and weak legs, five hearts feeling heat, bitter mouth with dry tongue, mental fatigue, excessive sleepiness, decreased appetite, and a weak, rapid pulse.
    1 qian each of Angelica, White Peony, Prepared Rehmannia, White Poria, and Ophiopogon,
    7 fen each of Aged Tangerine Peel, Anemarrhena, and fried Coptis,
    5 fen of Ginseng, 3 fen of Licorice, 10 seeds of Schisandra.
    Grind the above into a powder, prepare with one jujube, a pinch of rice, and boil in water for consumption.

    Qingqi Yin (Clear Qi Drink)
    Used to treat summer-induced damage to the original energy, showing symptoms of fever, excessive sweating, lack of strength, and a weak, fine, slow pulse.
    1 qian and 2 fen of Atractylodes, 1 qian each of Ginseng, Astragalus, Ophiopogon, White Peony, Aged Tangerine Peel, and White Poria,
    7 fen each of Anemarrhena and Perilla,
    5 fen each of fried Coptis and Licorice,
    3 fen of Coptis.
    Grind the above, add three slices of ginger, and boil in water for medicinal use.[99p]
    Herbal Formulas for Heat Relief and Vitality:

    1. Shen Ru Beverage (also known as "Cooling and Strengthening Brew"): Used to drive away heat, clear heat, boost vitality, and treat symptoms like vomiting and diarrhea caused by summer heat.

      • Ingredients:
        • 1.5 coins of Atractylodes
        • 1.2 coins of Ginseng
        • 1 coin each of Ophiopogon, White Peony, and White Poria
        • 0.7 coins each of Anemarrhena and Dried Tangerine Peel
        • 0.5 coins of Licorice
        • 0.3 coins of stir-fried Scutellaria
        • 10 pieces of Schisandra
      • Method: Prepare with three pieces of ginger, boil in water, and take as medicine.

    2. Huang Qi Decoction: Used for treating heat-induced thirst and weakness of the pulse.

      • Ingredients:
        • 1 coin each of Ginseng, stir-fried Atractylodes, White Poria, Licorice, and honey-fried Astragalus
        • 1 coin of White Peony
      • Method: Process with three pieces of ginger, boil in water, and take as medicine.

    3. Cooling and Vitality-Enhancing Brew (from the "Ten Treatises of Dong Yuan"): Treats prolonged summer heat dampening the body, fatigue in the limbs, lack of energy, reluctance to move, body heat with thirst, frequent yellow urination, loose stool, desire to eat lost, shortness of breath, and spontaneous sweating.

      • Ingredients:
        • 1.5 coins of Atractylodes,
        • 1 coin each of Huang Qi and Cimicifuga,
        • 0.5 coins each of Ginseng, Atractylodes, Dried Tangerine Peel, and Fermented Barley,
        • 0.3 coins each of Wine Yellow Bile, Angelica, Green Tangerine Peel, Ophiopogon, Kudzu, and Licorice,
        • 9 pieces of Schisandra.
      • Method: Grind together into one batch, boil in water, and take as medicine. This medicine features Ginseng, Atractylodes, Astragalus, Cimicifuga, Licorice, Ophiopogon, Angelica, Schisandra, Wine Yellow Bile, and Kudzu to clear heat and replenish vitality; while Atractylodes, Fermented Barley, Dried Tangerine Peel, Green Tangerine Peel, and ZeXie work to regulate the spleen.[100p]
